class="css-6n7j50"><button aria-expanded="false" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="More sharing options" class="css-16ogagc" data-testid=""><svg class="css-uhuo44" height="13" viewbox="0 0 16 13" fill-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></button></div></li><li class="css-60hakz"></li></ul></div></div></li></ul></div></div></header><section class="meteredContent css-1i2y565" itemprop="articleBody" name="articleBody"><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">WASHINGTON — Supreme Court justices insist that politics plays no role in their decision-making. But <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2018/06/27/us/politics/kennedy-retirement-supreme-court-median.html?module=inline" title="">their voting patterns</a> and the <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/10/06/us/politics/conservative-supreme-court-kavanaugh.html?module=inline" title="">titanic partisan confirmation battles</a> for seats on the court tell a different story.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“We don’t work as Democrats or Republicans,” Chief Justice John G. Roberts Jr. <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/03/22/us/politics/john-roberts-criticized-supreme-court-confirmation-process-before-there-was-a-vacancy.html?module=inline" title="">said in 2016</a>, not long before the start of <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/06/14/us/politics/obama-supreme-court-merrick-garland.html?module=inline" title="">a successful 10-month Republican blockade</a> of President Barack Obama’s nomination of Judge Merrick B. Garland. The senators who refused to give Judge Garland a hearing appeared to disagree with the chief justice’s assessment.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In the end, President Trump filled the vacancy left by the death of Justice Antonin Scalia <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/01/31/us/politics/neil-gorsuch-supreme-court-nominee.html?module=inline" title="">with Justice Neil M. Gorsuch</a>. At <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.congress.gov/115/chrg/shrg28638/CHRG-115shrg28638.htm" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">his confirmation hearing</a>, Justice Gorsuch echoed the chief justice.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“I do not see Republican judges, and I do not see Democrat judges,” he said. “I see judges.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Trump sees things differently. “We need more Republicans in 2018 and must ALWAYS hold the Supreme Court!” <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://twitter.com/realdonaldtrump/status/978932860307505153?lang=en" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">he tweeted</a> about a year after Justice Gorsuch took his seat.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Political science data tends to support the politicians rather than the justices when it comes to whether politics plays a part in judges’ decisions. <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.hup.harvard.edu/catalog.php?isbn=9780674049895" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">The data</a> demonstrates a significant correlation between judges’ political affiliations and their voting.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Some Democrats have floated proposals to take into account that reality. Pete Buttigieg, the mayor of South Bend, Ind., and a 2020 presidential candidate, for instance, has outlined a plan based on <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3288958" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a paper from two law professors</a>, Daniel Epps of Washington University in St. Louis and Ganesh Sitaraman of Vanderbilt, to mandate ideological balance on the Supreme Court. Under that proposal, the number of justices would grow to 15 from nine: five affiliated with the Republican Party, five affiliated with the Democratic Party and five others selected by the first 10 based on their “fairness, independence and centrism.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Such proposals <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/06/opinion/buttigieg-warren-supreme-court.html?module=inline" title="">face practical hurdles and constitutional objections</a>. But, particularly in light of <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/10/06/us/politics/brett-kavanaugh-supreme-court.html?module=inline" title="">the stormy confirmation hearings last year</a> for Justice Brett M. Kavanaugh and their lingering aftershocks, there are reasons to think that ideological balance would be good for the court.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Several justices <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/04/05/us/politics/supreme-court-is-working-hard-to-avoid-deadlocks-elena-kagan-says.html?module=inline" title="">have said that they worked much harder</a> to come together in the year that they were short-handed after <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/02/14/us/antonin-scalia-death.html?module=inline" title="">Justice Scalia’s death</a>. That eight-member court included four Republican appointees and four Democratic ones. It <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/06/27/us/politics/supreme-court-term-consensus.html?module=inline" title="">reached a modern record for consensus</a>.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The debate over the role politics plays in judging is mostly theoretical. But <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/19/19A57/107785/20190711114004325_Governor%20of%20Delaware%20v%20Adams%20Application%20for%20an%20Extension%20of%20Time.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a petition</a> filed this month by Gov. John C. Carney Jr. of Delaware, a Democrat, makes it concrete. It asks the justices to consider whether states may take account of the political affiliations of judges to try to achieve something like ideological balance on their courts.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The court will decide whether to hear the case, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/search.aspx?filename=/docket/docketfiles/html/public/19-309.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Carney v. Adams</a>, No. 19-309, sometime this fall.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Delaware’s Constitution says that judges affiliated with any one political party can make up no more than a “bare majority” on the state’s highest courts, with the remaining seats reserved for judges affiliated with the “other major political party.”</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/16/us/politics/16dc-bar2/16dc-bar2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><img alt="Gov. John C. Carney Jr. of Delaware. The state’s Constitution says that judges affiliated with any one political party can make up no more than a “bare majority” on the state’s highest courts." class="css-1m50asq" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/16/us/politics/16dc-bar2/16dc-bar2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" itemprop="url" sizes="((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 60vw, 100vw" src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/16/us/politics/16dc-bar2/16dc-bar2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" srcset="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/16/us/politics/16dc-bar2/16dc-bar2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/16/us/politics/16dc-bar2/16dc-bar2-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/16/us/politics/16dc-bar2/16dc-bar2-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w"/></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">Gov. John C. Carney Jr. of Delaware. The state’s Constitution says that judges affiliated with any one political party can make up no more than a “bare majority” on the state’s highest courts.</span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>Jason Minto/The News Journal, via Associated Press</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">James R. Adams, a retired lawyer and registered independent, challenged the balancing provision, saying it violated the First Amendment.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“If Delaware had a Constitution that said, you know, a certain race or religion or gender was eliminated from being judges, everyone would say that’s obviously discrimination,” he said in <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/6401526-Adams-Sj.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a deposition</a>. Since he was an independent, he said, he was ineligible to be considered for a seat on the state’s three highest courts.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“It’s kind of like those old discrimination cases about whether you’re in the front of the bus or the back of the bus,” Mr. Adams said. “The Delaware Constitution has decided that sometimes independents are allowed in the back of the bus and sometimes they can’t get on the bus.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">David L. Finger, a lawyer for Mr. Adams, said the balancing requirement was an insult to judicial independence. “It assumes,” he said, “that judges cannot put aside their political philosophies to decide the cases before them.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, in Philadelphia, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www2.ca3.uscourts.gov/opinarch/181045p.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">ruled for Mr. Adams</a>, striking down the balancing requirement.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Supreme Court precedents, the appeals court said, have drawn a line. On the one hand, politics may play no role in the hiring and firing of most government workers. On the other, it is perfectly acceptable to consider politics for those in “policymaking positions.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In other words, it is unlawful to hire, say, prison guards or tow-truck drivers based on their political affiliations. But it is fine to consider politics in the appointment of officials who have a role in making policy like, say, prosecutors and public information officers.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Which side of the line do judges fall on?</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.fjc.gov/history/judges/fuentes-julio-m" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Judge Julio M. Fuentes</a>, writing for a unanimous three-member panel of the Third Circuit, said judges were in the first category.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“Judges are not policymakers,” <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www2.ca3.uscourts.gov/opinarch/181045ppan.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">he wrote</a>, “because whatever decisions judges make in any given case relates to the case under review and not to partisan political interests.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">That was at odds with statements from other appeals courts, which may have taken a more realistic view of what judges actually do. “A judge both makes and implements governmental policy,” <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.fjc.gov/history/judges/easterbrook-frank-hoover" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Judge Frank H. Easterbrook</a> of the Seventh Circuit, in Chicago, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://casetext.com/case/kurowski-v-krajewski" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">wrote in 1988</a>. Five years later, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.fjc.gov/history/judges/keith-damon-jerome" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Judge Damon J. Keith</a> of the Sixth Circuit, in Cincinnati, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://law.justia.com/cases/federal/appellate-courts/F2/986/159/101992/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">agreed</a>. “Judges are policymakers because their political beliefs influence and dictate their decisions on important jurisprudential matters,” he wrote.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The lead lawyer for Mr. Carney is <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://law.stanford.edu/directory/michael-w-mcconnell/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Michael W. McConnell,</a> a prominent former federal appeals court judge who is now a law professor at Stanford. He said the justices who have insisted that there is no political component in judging had made “aspirational statements.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“I really approve of the aspirational statements,” he added, a little wistfully.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div></section><div class="bottom-of-article"><div class="css-1ubp8k9"></div><div class="css-1yif149"> to the Court’s Image</h2></div><time class="css-sehbwy e16638kd0" datetime="2018-10-06T11:15:21-04:00">Oct. 6, 2018</time></div></div><div class="css-161i88g"><figure aria-label="media" class="" class="css-dg19va"><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70"></p><div class="css-ip4ck2"><div><h2 class="css-65zpa8 eoo0vm40">A Bitter Nominee, Questions of Neutrality, and a Damaged Supreme Court</h2></div><time class="css-sehbwy e16638kd0" datetime="2018-09-28T14:06:36-04:00">Sept. 28, 2018</time></div></div><div class="css-161i88g"><figure aria-label="media" class="" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/09/29/us/politics/29dc-court-alpha/29dc-court-alpha-thumbLarge.jpg" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div><span class="css-1dv1kvn">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:386.6666666666667px"></div></div></div><figcaption class="css-17ai7jg emkp2hg0" itemprop="caption description"></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1i15vd3"></div></a><a class="css-ljapoq" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2012/11/27/us/judges-rulings-follow-partisan-lines.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedCoverage&amp;pgtype=Article&amp;region=Footer"><div class="css-cmgnk4"><div class="css-dg19va"><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70"></p><div class="css-ip4ck2"><div><h2 class="css-65zpa8 eoo0vm40">‘Politicians in Robes’? Not Exactly, But . . .</h2></div><time class="css-sehbwy e16638kd0" datetime="2012-11-26T14:07:25-05:00">Nov. 26, 2012</time></div></div><div class="css-161i88g"><figure aria-label="media" class="" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2012/11/27/us/BAR1/BAR1-thumbLarge.jpg" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div><span class="css-1dv1kvn">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:386.6666666666667px"></div></div></div><figcaption class="css-17ai7jg emkp2hg0" itemprop="caption description"></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-nn5nd0"></div></a></div></div><div></div><div></div><div><div class="css-1ede5it" id="bottom-wrapper"><div class="css-l9onyx" id="bottom-slug"><p>Advertisement</p></div><div class="ad bottom-wrapper" style="text-align:center;height:100%;display:block;min-height:90px"><div class="" data-position="bottom" id="bottom"></div></div></div></div></article></div></main><nav aria-labelledby="site-index-label" class="css-1ropbjl" data-testid="site-index" id="site-index"><div class="css-uw59u"><header class="css-jxzr5i" data-testid="site-index-header"><h2 class="css-vz7hjd" id="site-index-label">Site Index</h2><a href="/"><svg class="css-oylsik" fill="#000" viewbox="0 0 184 25" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg"><path d="M13.8 2.9c0-2-1.9-2.5-3.4-2.5v.3c.9 0 1.6.3 1.6 1 0 .4-.3 1-1.2 1-.7 0-2.2-.4-3.3-.8C6.2 1.4 5 1 4 1 2 1 .6 2.5.6 4.2c0 1.5 1.1 2 1.5 2.2l.1-.2c-.2-.2-.5-.4-.5-1 0-.4.4-1.1 1.4-1.1.9 0 2.1.4 3.7.9 1.4.4 2.9.7 3.7.8v3.1L9 10.2v.1l1.5 1.3v4.3c-.8.5-1.7.6-2.5.6-1.5 0-2.8-.4-3.9-1.6l4.1-2V6l-5 2.2C3.6 6.9 4.7 6 5.8 5.4l-.1-.3c-3 .8-5.7
